tips!</p>\n</div>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/alberta-road-trip/\">How to See Alberta: A 10-Day Suggested Driving Itinerary</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com\">Nomadic Matt&#039;s Travel Site</a>.</p>\n","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 06 Jul 2020 09:19:02 +0000","feedId":55,"bgimg":"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta1a.jpg","linkMd5":"88754485b9b00bc8a642c7836d30f943","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn20@2020_2/2020-07-30/1596109818550_e3a5800928e9845c.webp","author":"NomadicMatt","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta1a.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn20@2020_2/2020-07-30/1596109818550_e3a5800928e9845c.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta1.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn16@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596109830862_d7b18e56e16fb188.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta2.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn36@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596109830532_c2b5e027a496d37b.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta3.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn66@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596109829846_424365da719028c9.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn74@2020_2/2020-07-30/1596109829898_5e502f5dd956840f.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta5.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn16@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596109829262_e085ae4dccc1cb64.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta6.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn36@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596109828893_19c5df78d2c28579.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta7.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn87@2020_5/2020-07-30/1596109828559_1a62388190d5eef9.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/alberta8.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn4@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596109834381_a56e6ab9f14a25a6.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:50:09","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:50:09","title":"A 7-Day Southern California Road Trip Itinerary","link":"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/?p=206779","description":"<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad1d.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"An iconic Joshua Tree from Joshua Tree National Park in California, USA\"><br />\n<strong>Posted</strong>: 6/29/20 | June 29th, 2020</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/united-states-travel-costs/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">I’ve been on many road trips around the US</a> over the years. It’s by far my favorite way to explore the country. </p>\n<p>One state in particular is perfect for road-tripping: California.</p>\n<p>Occupying much of the West Coast, California is home to almost 40 million people. Here you find high mountains, giant trees, deserts, valleys, stunning beaches, and vibrant cities. It’s its own little country in some ways.  </p>\n<p>Since California is so big, I’m going to do a series of posts that cover road trips in the state as it’s better to focus on smaller areas so you can really soak up and enjoy each destination. In travel, less is more. </p>\n<p>To kick off the series, here is a one-week itinerary for Southern California:<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>Days 1–2: Los Angeles</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad2.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"Looking out over Los Angeles from behind the Hollywood sign\"><br />\nStart in Los Angeles, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/loving-los-angeles/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">a city I’ve come to love</a>. It’s not a city for tourists. Everything is spread out, you need a car, and the traffic makes getting around a pain. But, if you can enjoy its slow pace, diverse food and drink scene, and hikes, you’ll get a taste of what it’s like to live there. You visit LA like how you would spend your Saturday. </p>\n<p>The city has a lot to do. Here are some of my personal favorite activities:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>Stroll down Hollywood Boulevard</strong> – Don’t miss the Walk of Fame (where celebrities have their names engraved in the sidewalk) and Grauman’s Chinese Theatre (featuring celebrities’ handprints and footprints). </li>\n<li><strong>Visit LACMA</strong> &#8211;  With over 150,000 works, the Los Angeles County Museum of Art is the largest art museum in the western USA. They have collections from pretty much every era throughout history and every region of the world. Admission is $25 USD.</li>\n<li><strong>Hike to the Hollywood Sign</strong> – While you can snap pictures of the sign from pretty much anywhere in the city, it’s also possible to hike up to the sign itself to take in the view. The three trails (from easiest to hardest) are the Mt. Hollywood Trail, the Brush Canyon Trail, and the Cahuenga Peak Trail.</li>\n<li><strong>Browse The Last Bookstore</strong> – This is one of my favorite bookstores in the world. It sells books and records, has art displays, and features a cool upstairs area too with cheap books. Browse the shelves, grab a coffee, and buy a book!</li>\n<li><strong>Hit the beach</strong> – There are a lot of awesome beaches in and around Los Angeles. Some of the best are: Venice Beach, Carbon Beach, Santa Monica State Beach, Huntington City Beach, and El Matador.</li>\n<li><strong>Visit the Getty Museum</strong> – Opened in 1997 as part of the massive Getty Center, this museum has a diverse collection of paintings, manuscripts, drawings, and other artwork from the eighth century to present day. Admission is free.</li>\n<li><strong>Go hiking</strong> – LA has a lot of hiking trails so it’s easy to connect with nature. Some worth checking out are the Charlie Turner Trail (90 minutes), Baldwin Hills (30 minutes), Runyon Canyon (45 minutes), Portuguese Bend Reserve (3 hours), and Echo Mountain (3–3.5 hours).</li>\n<li><strong>See the Le Brea Tar Pits</strong> – These natural asphalt pits are located in Hancock Park and have existed for upwards of 50,000 years. Tons of fossils, preserved for centuries, have been found in them. The museum has lots of interesting information about the pits and how they came to be.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more things to see and do, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-guides/united-states-travel-guide/los-angeles/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">here’s my complete guide to Los Angeles</a>.</p>\n<p>Los Angeles also has countless food options. Some places I love are Musso &#038; Frank Grill, Dan Tana’s, Meals by Genet, The Butcher’s Daughter, Sugarfish, and Thai Pepper. </p>\n<p><strong>Where to Stay</strong></p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj/destination:https%3A%2F%2Fwww.hostelworld.com%2Fhosteldetails.php%2FBanana-Bungalow-Hollywood%2FLos-Angeles%2F11182\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Banana Bungalow Hollywood</a> – A chill, laid-back hostel that’s super social and organizes lots of activities.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj/destination:https%3A%2F%2Fwww.hostelworld.com%2Fhosteldetails.php%2FFreehand-Los-Angeles%2FLos-Angeles%2F272919\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Freehand Los Angeles</a> – This hostel/hotel features designer rooms with comfortable beds, a rooftop pool and bar with amazing views of the city, a lobby bar, a restaurant, and even a fitness center. </li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more suggestions, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/best-hostels-in-los-angeles/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">here’s my complete list of favorite hostels in Los Angeles!</a><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>Days 2–3: San Diego </h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/sandiegohostel2.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The skyline of San Diego as seen from over the bay\"><br />\nTwo hours down the coast is San Diego. It’s a popular city for sure but not as popular as the others. Personally, after LA, it’s my second favorite spot in the state! It’s easier to navigate, cheaper, has amazing weather, awesome beaches, and plenty of great bars and restaurants. Spend two nights here. </p>\n<p>Here are some things to do during your visit:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>Visit the USS Midway Museum</strong> – This aircraft carrier was the largest ship in the world until 1955. It saw action in numerous conflicts before being decommissioned in 1992 and turned into a museum. You can explore the flight deck as well as many of the rooms below.</li>\n<li><strong>Have fun at Belmont Park</strong> – This kitschy amusement park is right next to the ocean and perfect for some cheesy fun. It has a few classic rides as well as games and lots of greasy (and delicious) food and snacks.</li>\n<li><strong>Go surfing</strong> – Whether you’re a veteran or a newbie, grab a board and hit the waves. There’s some awesome surfing here. You can usually rent a board for around $30 USD a day. 90-minute lessons cost around $70 USD.</li>\n<li><strong>Visit the San Diego Zoo</strong> – Located in Balboa Park and with over 3,500 animals and 700,000 plant species, the San Diego Zoo is a massive, 1,800-acre park where you could easily spend an entire day. It’s one of the best zoos in the country and a great choice for anyone traveling with kids.</li>\n<li><strong>Explore Balboa Park</strong> – This is one of the oldest recreational parks in the US. In addition to the zoo, there are dozens of museums, as well as gardens, walking paths, sports fields, stadiums, theatres, and more. It’s a massive cultural center and green space with a ton to see and do.</li>\n<li><strong>Enjoy Pacific Beach</strong> – Head to Pacific Beach if you want to soak up the sun, surf, or go for a swim. The area is also known for its nightlife and offers lots of bars, clubs, and restaurants too.</li>\n<li><strong>Go whale watching</strong> – Between December and April, California gray whales migrate from Alaska to Mexico. They can grow up to 49 feet long and are incredible to see up close. Tours usually cost around $35 USD.</li>\n<li><strong>Hike Point Loma</strong> – This peninsula is where Europeans first arrived in California. You can walk out to the tip and enjoy the sweeping views of the ocean, visit the old lighthouse (built in 1855), and watch locals climb the cliffs of Osprey Point.</li>\n</ul>\n<p><strong>Where to Stay</strong></p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj/destination:https%3A%2F%2Fwww.hostelworld.com%2Fhosteldetails.php%2FHI-San-Diego-Downtown%2FSan-Diego%2F1856\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">HI San Diego</a> – This hostel organizes tons of events and tours, includes free breakfast, and has a big kitchen so you can cook your own food to save money. </li>\n<li><a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj/destination:https%3A%2F%2Fwww.hostelworld.com%2Fhosteldetails.php%2FITH-Adventure-Hostel-San-Diego%2FSan-Diego%2F66491%3FdateFrom%3D2020-08-26%26dateTo%3D2020-08-27%26number_of_guests%3D1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">ITH Adventure Hostel</a> – This eco-hostel has a vegetable garden (and gives free veggies to guests), a recycling and compost program, and even backyard chickens. There is lots of outdoor common space to relax in too.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>For more suggestions, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/best-hostels-san-diego/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">here’s a list of my favorite hostels in San Diego!</a><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>Days 3–5: Joshua Tree National Park</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad3.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The iconic Joshua trees growing in the aria California desert\"><br />\nSandwiched between the Mojave Desert and the Colorado Desert, Joshua Tree National Park offers some of the most incredible landscapes in the country. Swaths of cacti and towering boulders dot the arid panorama, all peppered by the famous Joshua trees and their distinct twisting trunks and branches. </p>\n<p>It’s an otherworldly place, a Martian landscape perfect for hiking, camping, and escaping the busy cities along California’s coast. There are countless trails to enjoy, ranging from short and easy to multiday challenges. Some suggestions:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>Ryan Mountain</strong> – A steep 3-mile hike offering some incredible views.</li>\n<li><strong>Wall Street Mill</strong> – An easy 2.8-mile hike that leads to an old mill used to refine ore during from gold mining.</li>\n<li><strong>Split Rock Loop</strong> – A quiet 2-mile hike with lots of neat rock formations.</li>\n<li><strong>Barker Dam Trail</strong> – A 1.1-mile loop offering a chance to see rabbits, bighorn sheep, and all kinds of birds.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>The visitor center has more information and trail maps. It’s only $30 USD for a seven-day vehicle pass, which is good for multiple entries in case you stay outside the park itself. The park is three hours from San Diego.</p>\n<p><strong>Where to Stay</strong><br />\n<a href=\"http://airbnb.7eer.net/c/214481/264339/4273\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Airbnb</a> is the best option here if you don’t have your own camping gear, although there are also glamping and more rustic options around the area too.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>Days 5–7: Sequoia National Park &#038; Kings Canyon National Park</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad4.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"Massive redwood and sequoia trees in Sequoia National park in California, USA\"><br />\nStart early and head to Sequoia National Park. It’s a long drive from Joshua Tree (it takes about 4-6 hours to get there). </p>\n<p>The park itself is comprised of towering redwood and Sequoia trees, some of the biggest trees in the world. I’m talking trees so thick you can drive a car though them and so tall that you can’t see their crown! </p>\n<p>Start things off by visiting the Giant Forest Museum to learn about the geography, history, and importance of the park. Afterward, walk the Big Trees Trail, a short loop that will get you in and amongst the trees. It also has the largest single-stem tree in the world, General Sherman!</p>\n<p>For a sweeping view of the forest, hike up Moro Rock. Standing 250 feet tall, it’s a massive granite dome that juts out of the surrounding hills and forest. Stairs and a viewpoint were built into the rock itself so you can climb to the top safely and enjoy the magnificent vista.</p>\n<p>While you’re here, visit nearby Kings Canyon National Park, a mountainous park with beautiful lakes, valleys to hike, and spectacular views. It’s also where you can see “General Grant” (the third largest tree in the world). For a scenic drive, cruise the Kings Canyon Scenic Byway, a narrow road along the side of a mountain that offers more picturesque views of the rugged landscape. </p>\n<p><strong>Where to Stay</strong><br />\nThere are lots of places to camp here, both inside and outside of the parks. However, if camping isn’t your thing (it’s not really mine), there are plenty of lodges and hotels in the area. You can find them via <a href=\"http://www.booking.com/index.html?aid=362916\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Booking.com</a>.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>Day 7: Head back to Los Angeles</h3>\n<p>After that, it’s back to LA. It’s only a 3.5-hour drive, so if you’re not in a hurry, enjoy the desert views and stop along the way if you see anything that piques your interest.</p>\n<p><center>***</center></p>\n<p>California is a huge state with unlimited road-tripping potential. And, while there are a million different routes you could take for a seven-day trip, I think this itinerary covers some of the best of SoCal.</p>\n<div class=\"single-booking\">\n<h4>Book Your Trip to the USA: Logistical Tips and Tricks</h4>\n<p><strong>Book Your Flight</strong><br />\nFind a cheap flight by using <a href=\"http://www.anrdoezrs.net/click-3032045-12837948\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Skyscanner</a> or <a href=\"https://www.momondo.com/\">Momondo</a>. They are my two favorite search engines because they search websites and airlines around the globe so you always know no stone is left unturned.</p>\n<p><strong>Book Your Accommodation</strong><br />\nYou can book your hostel with <a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Hostelworld</a>. If you want to stay elsewhere, use <a href=\"https://www.booking.com/index.html?aid=362916\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Booking.com</a> as they consistently return the cheapest rates for guesthouses and cheap hotels. </p>\n<p><strong>Don&#8217;t Forget Travel Insurance</strong><br />\nTravel insurance will protect you against illness, injury, theft, and cancellations. It&#8217;s comprehensive protection in case anything goes wrong. I never go on a trip without it as I&#8217;ve had to use it many times in the past. I&#8217;ve been using <a href=\"https://www.worldnomads.com/lovedby/nomadic-matt?affiliate=nmts&#38;subId=brandlink&#38;affiliateTrackingId=0fe898ceef264503a88f74f4bbc7bb5b&#38;utm_source=nmts&#38;utm_medium=affiliate&#38;utm_campaign=Affiliate&#38;utm_content=brandlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads</a> for ten years. My favorite companies that offer the best service and value are:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.worldnomads.com/lovedby/nomadic-matt?affiliate=nmts&#38;subId=brandlink&#38;affiliateTrackingId=0fe898ceef264503a88f74f4bbc7bb5b&#38;utm_source=nmts&#38;utm_medium=affiliate&#38;utm_campaign=Affiliate&#38;utm_content=brandlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads</a> (for everyone below 70)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.insuremytrip.com/?linkid=GB841615\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Insure My Trip</a> (for those over 70)</li>\n</ul>\n<p><strong>Looking for the best companies to save money with?</strong><br />\nCheck out my <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-resources/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">resource page</a> for the best companies to use when you travel! I list all the ones I use to save money when I travel &#8211; and I think will help you too!</p>\n<p><strong>Want More Information on traveling the United States?</strong><br />\nBe sure to visit our your visit!</p>\n</div>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/california-road-trip-one-week-itinerary/\">A 7-Day Southern California Road Trip Itinerary</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com\">Nomadic Matt&#039;s Travel Site</a>.</p>\n","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 29 Jun 2020 09:19:37 +0000","feedId":55,"bgimg":"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad1d.jpg","linkMd5":"df1a4cfaf32b82903837bddcb01d07db","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn4@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596109818581_ada6949ccffade6c.webp","author":"NomadicMatt","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad1d.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn4@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596109818581_ada6949ccffade6c.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad2.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn98@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596109829953_335ef549c7c14540.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/sandiegohostel2.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn4@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596109830065_e41961470d372a0f.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad3.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn99@2020_6/2020-07-30/1596109828960_7ed9113e26b03b7c.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/californiaroad4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn8@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596109828839_2ea3e9c043166f68.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:50:09","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:50:09","title":"12 Things I&#8217;d Tell Any New Traveler","link":"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/?p=29641","description":"<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/newtraveler3.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"A solo traveler standing on a mountain looking out into the distance\"><br />\n<strong>Updated</strong>: 7/10/20 | July 10th, 2020 (Originally published 3/30/15)</p>\n<p>Hope. Fear. Excitement. Traveling for the first time produced a wave of emotions. </p>\n<p>When I left to travel the world on my first <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-tips/buying-a-rtw-ticket/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">round-the-world trip</a>, I didn’t know what to expect. </p>\n<p>Now, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/ten-years-a-nomad/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">with fifteen years of travel experience under my belt</a>, I know better. Traveling is second nature to me now. I land in an airport and I just go on autopilot. </p>\n<p>But, back then, I was as green as they come. </p>\n<p>To compensate for my lack of experience, I followed my guidebooks and wet my feet by going on organized tours. I was young and inexperienced and I made a lot of <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/not-to-do/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">rookie travel mistakes</a>.</p>\n<p>I know what it&#8217;s like to just be starting out and have a mind filled with questions, anxieties, and concerns. </p>\n<p>So, if you&#8217;re new to travel and looking for advice to help you prepare, here are 12 tips that I&#8217;d tell a new traveler to help them avoid some of my early mistakes:<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>1. Don’t Be Scared</h3>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/how-to-overcome-your-fears/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Fear is a powerful deterrent</a>. Taking the leap into the unknown is scary, but remember: you aren’t the first person to travel the world. You aren’t discovering new continents or exploring uncharted territories.</p>\n<p>There is a well-worn travel trail out <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/introducing-the-nomadic-network/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">there and people to help guide you along the way</a>. If millions of people can make their way around the world each year, you can too.</p>\n<p>You’re just as capable as anyone else. After all, you did the hardest part: deciding to go. Having the courage to make that decision is the hardest part.</p>\n<p>You&#8217;ll make mistakes. Everyone does. But that&#8217;s part of the experience.</p>\n<p>There will be lots of people out there to help you. You&#8217;ll be shocked at just how helpful and kind people are. You&#8217;ll make friends, you&#8217;ll survive, and you&#8217;ll be better for it.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>2. Don’t Live by Your Guidebook</h3>\n<p>Guidebooks are useful for a general overview of a destination. They&#8217;re a great way to learn the basics and get introduced to the cities and countries you plan to visit. But you’ll never find the latest off-the-beaten-path attractions, bars, or restaurants in them. </p>\n<p>For the latest info (as well as insider tips), connect with locals. Use websites like <a href=\"http://meetup.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Meetup.com</a> or <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/how-to-crush-it-on-couchsurfing/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Couchsurfing</a> to connect directly with local and expats so you can get suggesitons, advice, and tips to make the most of your trip.</p>\n<p>Additionally, ask other travelers you meet or the staff at your hotel/hostel. Visit the local tourist board as well. It&#8217;s a wealth of information that often gets overlooked.</p>\n<p>In short, use a guidebook for the foundation of your plans but fill in the details with up-to-date info from locals. </p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/my-current-list-of-favorite-blogs/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">You can also use travel blogs for planning tips</a> since they are updated more often than guidebooks.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>3. Travel Slow</h3>\n<p>This is something most new long-term travelers learn the hard way (myself included).</p>\n<p>I know it can be tempting to pack in as many cities and activities as possible. (This is especially true if you only have a few weeks of vacation.)</p>\n<p>But rushing from city to city every other day is just going to leave you exhausted and stressed out. You&#8217;ll experience a whirlwind of activity, most of which will remain a blur when you look back on it. Sure, you&#8217;ll have some great pictures for Instagram but is that really why you&#8217;re traveling?</p>\n<p>Travel is about quality, not quantity. Don&#8217;t worry about how much you see. Don&#8217;t worry about trying to impress people with the number of countries you&#8217;ve visited. Slow down and soak up your destinations. You&#8217;ll learn more, enjoy it more, and have a much more memorable experience.</p>\n<p>When it comes to travel, less is more. (Plus, traveling slow helps reduce your transportation costs. It&#8217;s cheaper to go slow!)</p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<h3>4. Pack Light</h3>\n<p>When I went to <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-guides/costa-rica-travel-tips/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Costa Rica</a> in 2003, I brought a bag filled with tons of stuff: hiking boots and pants, a fleece jacket, too much clothing, and my bodyweight in toiletries. And it all sat in my bag, mostly unused.</p>\n<p>I was packing for &#8220;just in case&#8221; and &#8220;what if&#8221; instead of the reality of my trip.</p>\n<p>While it can be tempting to bring more than you need &#8220;just in case,&#8221; remember this: you can buy things on the road. Socks, shampoo, jackets, new shoes — you can find it all aborad. There&#8217;s no need to bring everything and the kitchen sink. </p>\n<p>So, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/packing/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">pack light</a>. You’ll have less to carry, saving you the hassle and stress of lugging a huge backpack around for weeks (or months) on end.</p>\n<p>Unless you are going somewhere cold, a bag around 40 liters will suffice. Bags around this size are easier to carry, don&#8217;t get too unwieldy, and can fit on your flight as carry-on only if need be (a huge perk if you want to save yourself some headaches).</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-tips/choosing-the-right-backpack/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Here&#8217;s everything you need to know to help you find the perfect bag for your budget and your trip</a>.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>5. Get Travel Insurance</h3>\n<p>Whether you&#8217;re a travel veteran or a brand new backpacker, don&#8217;t leave home without making sure you&#8217;re protected in case something goes wrong. As we learned during the COVID-19 pandemic, sudden emergencies can come out of nowhere. </p>\n<p>I&#8217;ve had my luggage lost. I popped an eardrum in Thailand. <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/getting-stabbed-in-colombia/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">I was knifed in Colombia</a>. </p>\n<p>I&#8217;ve had a friend break bones, need to be helicoptered out of the Amazon, or fly back due to a sudden death. </p>\n<p>Stuff happens. </p>\n<p>To ensure you&#8217;re protected, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/travel-insurance/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">buy travel insurance</a>.</p>\n<p>I never leave home without it because I know just how quickly things can go sideways.</p>\n<p>You never know what might happen. The road is filled with uncertainty. Make sure you&#8217;re protected. It will also give you peace of mind and help you travel with confidence.</p>\n<p>Here are a few posts worth reading. I know it&#8217;s not a fun or sexy topic, but it&#8217;s an important one!</p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/is-travel-insurance-worth-it/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Is Travel Insurance Worth it?</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/best-travel-insurance-companies/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">The 7 Best Travel Insurance Companies</a></li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/world-nomads-review/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads Insurance Review</a></li>\n</ul>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<h3>6. Bring a Phone (and Get Local SIM Cards)</h3>\n<p>Having a phone with data means you can look up directions on the fly, make reservations, and contact emergency services if something happens.</p>\n<p>Sure, there is free wifi pretty much everywhere these days so buying a local SIM card for data might seem like a waste of money (especially if you&#8217;re on a really, really tight budget) but having that immediate access to roaming data can be a lifesaver.</p>\n<p>If you&#8217;re from the US and traveling for less than 3 months, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/t-mobile-for-travelers/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">T-Mobile has reliable data plans</a>. Google Fi is another great option too.</p>\n<p>Additionally, having a phone makes it easier to connect and stay in touch with travelers you meet. </p>\n<p>Simply put: having a phone is really helpful in this day and age. </p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/travel-phone/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Just don&#8217;t stay glued to it all the time</a>.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>7. Go with the Flow</h3>\n<p>When every day is planned out and there are timetables to follow, you&#8217;ll get stressed. Very stressed. You&#8217;ll rush around and be unhappy if there are any glitches in your well-curated schedule.</p>\n<p>And there will be hiccups. And glitches. And all kinds of inconveniences, both major and minor. Life on the road doesn&#8217;t always go as planned — which is both fun and frustrating.</p>\n<p>When you plan too much, there’s no room to experience the happy accidents of travel. There&#8217;s no room for spontaneous choice, for incorporating new information and advice that you learn.</p>\n<p>When making your plan, make sure that it&#8217;s flexible. <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/going-with-the-flow/\"target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Learn to go with the flow</a>. Plan one or two activities and let the rest of the day happen. </p>\n<p>It’ll be a more enjoyable and less stressful experience. You’ll be surprised by what happens. </p>\n<p>Be flexible. Let life unfold the way it should.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>8. Bring Some Extra Money</h3>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/the-ultimate-guide-to-traveling-when-you-have-no-money/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Travel isn’t as expensive as many people think</a> but you still need to create a budget that means your needs. The secret to long term travel is smart money management. </p>\n<p>However, always <em>overestimate</em> the amount you need. You never know what might come up on the road. After all, you didn&#8217;t spend all that time saving every penny and staying home to skip those once in a lifetime activities? </p>\n<p>Maybe you want to try bungee jumping or you discover an amazing restaurant you can&#8217;t pass up. Or maybe you meet some cool people and decide to scrap your plan altogether.</p>\n<p>No matter how well you plan, something can always come up that will throw your budget out of synch.</p>\n<p>That&#8217;s fine. </p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/save-for-trip-advice/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Just leave home with a little extra</a>. If you&#8217;re planning says you&#8217;ll need $2,000, bring $2,500. It will give you a buffer for emergencies and spontaneity.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>9. Remember Everyone is in the Same Boat</h3>\n<p>It takes courage to talk to strangers when you&#8217;re new to travel, especially if you&#8217;re an introvert like me. What do you say? Can you just invite people to join you? What if you end up alone? </p>\n<p>These are all questions I had when I first started traveling. The good news? Everyone is in the same boat. All around you are other solo travelers looking for friends. They want to meet new people too.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/how-to-be-more-interesting-while-traveling/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">While there are a few tricks to learn to help you meet people</a>, it mostly just comes down to saying “hello” and taking that first step. Everything else will fall into place after that. You have nothing to lose and, in the process — this is how you&#8217;ll get over your shyness, make new friends, and get better at conversation. </p>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<h3>10. Be Adventurous</h3>\n<p>The only time we grow is when we&#8217;re outside of our comfort zones. And travel is about growth. That doesn&#8217;t mean you need to do dangerous things, but it does mean you need to push yourself beyond what you&#8217;re used to.</p>\n<p>Hiking, sky diving, eating new foods, camping, rock climbing, hitchhiking — whatever taking a risk looks like to you is 100% ok. Everyone has different interests and tolerance levels. Push yours. It may be scary and uncomfortable at the time, but you&#8217;ll be glad you did it later.</p>\n<p>Challenge yourself. Try new things. You’ll walk away more self-confident.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>11. It&#8217;s Ok to Change Your Mind</h3>\n<p>If you hate a city, leave and go to another one. If you don&#8217;t enjoy the tour your on, cancel it early. And if you really love the place you&#8217;re visiting, change your plans and stay longer. </p>\n<p>It&#8217;s perfectly normal to change your mind on the road.</p>\n<p>Maybe that means extending your trip. Maybe that means going home early. There&#8217;s nothing wrong with either choice.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/going-home/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Always remember you can go home if you aren’t having fun</a>. You aren’t stuck with your decision to travel or your decision to be in a specific place. You&#8217;re the captain on your own ship. Never forget that!<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>12. Remember, You&#8217;re Not Alone</h3>\n<p>Wherever you go, there is a network of travelers who will be your friends, give you advice or tips, and help you out. They will guide you, point you in the right direction, and be your mentors. </p>\n<p>You aren’t out there on your own. </p>\n<p>And you will be OK. </p>\n<p><center>***</center></p>\n<p>I know you’re nervous about heading out into the unknown. It’s human nature to worry. But, if you remember these words of wisdom, you&#8217;ll go into this the right mindset and able to avoid rookie mistakes.</p>\n<p>So take a deep breath, relax, and enjoy your trip!</p>\n<div class=\"single-booking\">\n<h4>Book Your Trip: Logistical Tips and Tricks</h4>\n<p><strong>Book Your Flight</strong><br />\nFind a cheap flight by using <a href=\"http://www.anrdoezrs.net/click-3032045-12837948\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Skyscanner</a> or <a href=\"https://www.momondo.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Momondo</a>. They are my two favorite search engines, because they search websites and airlines around the globe so you always know no stone is being left unturned. </p>\n<p><strong>Book Your Accommodation</strong><br />\nYou can book your hostel with <a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Hostelworld</a> as they have the largest inventory. If you want to stay somewhere other than a hostel, use <a href=\"https://www.booking.com/index.html?aid=362916\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Booking.com</a>, as they consistently return the cheapest rates for guesthouses and hotels. </p>\n<p><strong>Don&#8217;t Forget Travel Insurance</strong><br />\nTravel insurance will protect you against illness, injury, theft, and cancellations. It&#8217;s comprehensive protection in case anything goes wrong. I never go on a trip without it, as I&#8217;ve had to use it many times in the past. I&#8217;ve been using <a href=\"https://www.worldnomads.com/lovedby/nomadic-matt?affiliate=nmts&#38;subId=brandlink&#38;affiliateTrackingId=0fe898ceef264503a88f74f4bbc7bb5b&#38;utm_source=nmts&#38;utm_medium=affiliate&#38;utm_campaign=Affiliate&#38;utm_content=brandlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads</a> for ten years. My favorite companies that offer the best service and value are:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.worldnomads.com/lovedby/nomadic-matt?affiliate=nmts&#38;subId=brandlink&#38;affiliateTrackingId=0fe898ceef264503a88f74f4bbc7bb5b&#38;utm_source=nmts&#38;utm_medium=affiliate&#38;utm_campaign=Affiliate&#38;utm_content=brandlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads</a> (for everyone below 70)</li>\n<li><a href=https://www.insuremytrip.com/?linkId=5d1cb667a65d12041eca44ec\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Insure My Trip</a> (for those 70 and over)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://medjetassist.com/nomadicmatt\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Medjet</a> (for additional repatriation coverage)</li>\n</ul>\n<p><strong>Looking for the best companies to save money with?</strong><br />\nCheck out my <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-resources/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">resource page</a> for the best companies to use when you travel! I list all those I use — and they&#8217;ll save you time and money too! </p>\n</div>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/new-traveler-advice/\">12 Things I&#8217;d Tell Any New Traveler</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com\">Nomadic Matt&#039;s Travel Site</a>.</p>\n","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 10 Jul 2020 12:33:50 +0000","feedId":55,"bgimg":"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/newtraveler3.jpg","linkMd5":"733d5edf8678a459cf50bf594a26cc37","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn99@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596109818473_0ddbf0de0267c4ca.webp","author":"NomadicMatt","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/newtraveler3.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn99@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596109818473_0ddbf0de0267c4ca.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:50:09","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:50:09","title":"14 Things to See and Do in Detroit","link":"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/?p=207314","description":"<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/detroitthings1b.jpg\" alt=\"The downtown skyline of Detroit, Michigan during the summer\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\n<strong>Posted</strong>: 7/23/20 | July 23rd, 2020</p>\n<p><em><a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/coronavirus-travel-resources/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Since the Coronavirus has halted travel for over four months</a>, I thought I would start to share more posts about destinations here in the United States. True, we shouldn&#8217;t be doing a lot of travel at the moment but you can always use these tips for later! Today, my Creative Director Raimee of <a href=\"http://doitallabroad.com\">All Abroad</a> shares her tips and advice for visiting Detroit, one of the country&#8217;s most underrated cities!</em></p>\n<p>Just north of Lake Erie’s western end, Detroit, Michigan, is a sprawling metropolis home to over four million people. Haunted by the echoes of its past, the city is often overlooked or ignored by domestic and international travelers alike.</p>\n<p>Having grown up in the Detroit area, I can understand why those unaware of its charm consider Detroit a blighted city, burdened by debt, crime, and a fleeing population. I assure you, though, this preconception couldn’t be more wrong.</p>\n<p>The famed “Motor City” has historically been known for its auto manufacturing sector, its contributions to the early music industry, and its beloved sports teams. Today, through its revitalization, Detroit has taken on a new appeal.</p>\n<p>From its world-class museums and its incredible assortment of eateries to its culturally-inspired dive bars and eclectic garage-like music scene, Detroit is one of the most exciting cities in America to both explore and be a part of right now. Its population is motivated, its people are proud, and the suburbs’ rekindled interest in downtown has helped open the door to a new era of prosperity and a growing young population.</p>\n<p>To help inspire you to plan a trip, here is my curated list of things to see and do I’d recommend to anyone visiting Detroit:<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>1. Take a Free Walking Tour</h3>\n<p>Start your visit with a free walking tour. You’ll get an introduction to the city and its past, learn about its evolution and recent developments, and see the main downtown sights. You’ll also get access to an expert local guide who can answer all your questions.</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://detroitexperiencefactory.org/public-tours\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Detroit Experience Factory</a> offers daily free tours (as well as more in-depth paid tours) that will give you a solid introduction. Just make sure to tip your guide at the end!<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>2. Visit the Detroit Institute of Arts</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit10.jpg\" alt=\"The exterior of the Detroit Institute of Arts in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nThe Detroit Institute of Arts is a 130-year-old museum located in the heart of Midtown and has something to offer every visitor. There are more than 65,000 works of art here, ranging from classic to more modern and contemporary pieces, spread out over 100 different galleries. It&#8217;s a massive space!</p>\n<p>While you could easily spend hours here, if you choose your galleries in advance, you can be in and out in two hours without rushing.</p>\n<p><em>5200 Woodward Ave., +1 313-833-7900, dia.org. Open weekdays 9am–4pm (10pm on Fridays) and weekends 10am–5pm. Admission is $14 USD.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>3. Relax at Belle Isle</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit2.jpg\" alt=\"The conservatory on Belle Island near Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nYou could easily spend an entire day exploring Belle Isle, a 982-acre island park with a variety of activities and attractions. It’s a popular destination for locals to gather on a sunny day for picnics and barbeques, for hanging out at the beach, or for walking along its various nature trails.</p>\n<p>Here are some of my other favorite things to do at Belle Isle:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>Wander the conservatory</strong> – The Anna Scripps Whitcomb Conservatory is a peaceful botanical garden stretching over 13 acres, with dozens of walking paths and greenhouses to explore. Admission is free.</li>\n<li><strong>Hit the range</strong> – Belle Island Golf Range is driving range with practice areas for driving, putting, and chipping. A bucket of balls is just $5.50 USD.</li>\n<li><strong>Enjoy the beach</strong> – There’s over half a mile of beach where you can swim, lounge, or rent a kayak or paddleboard and soak up the sun.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<h3>4. Explore the Eastern Market</h3>\n<p>The Eastern Market is a huge marketplace with local foods, art, jewelry, artisan crafts, and more. It covers 43 acres and is the largest historic public market district in the <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-guides/united-states-travel-guide/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">United States</a>, dating back over 150 years.</p>\n<p>There are three different market days during the week: Saturdays, Sundays, and Tuesdays. It is particularly busy on Saturdays when farmers tend to bring in their poultry, livestock, and fresh produce for sale.</p>\n<p><em>2934 Russell St, +1 313-833-9300, easternmarket.org. Check the website for market days and times. Admission is free.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>5. Walk or Bike Along the Dequindre Cut</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit3.jpg\" alt=\"The paved path along the Dequindre Cut in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nThe Dequindre Cut Greenway is a two-mile urban recreational path that offers a pedestrian link between the East Riverfront, the Eastern Market, and several residential neighborhoods in between. Along the path, you’ll find all kinds of street art, as well as buskers in the summer. It’s a nice place to walk or jog and take in the city.</p>\n<p>If you plan on visiting the Eastern Market and the Riverfront (which you should!), consider renting a bike (they&#8217;re just $8 USD per day from mogodetroit.com).<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>6. Check Out One of the Largest Bookstores in the World</h3>\n<p>Maybe it’s because I love all bookstores, but this is one of my favorite places to explore in Detroit. John K. King Used &#38; Rare Books, located in an old glove factory, is an enchanting host to over one million books.</p>\n<p>I love spending time wandering through the rows of strange titles and marveling at the rare editions they have in stock — some are so rare, you have to make an appointment to be allowed to view them.</p>\n<p><em>901 W. Lafayette Blvd., +1 313-961-0622, johnkingbooksdetroit.com. Open Tuesday–Saturday 10am–5pm.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>7. Visit the Fox Theatre</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit4.jpg\" alt=\"The spacious and ornate interior of the Fox Theatre in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nThe Fox Theatre is the largest surviving movie palace of the 1920s. Built in 1928, and with over 5,000 seats, it continues to host a variety of live productions and events (like concerts, standup comedy, and children’s performances).</p>\n<p>The building is a National Historic Landmark, the highest honor given by the National Park Service, and is open for tours in case you can’t catch a performance during your trip. The interior is absolutely stunning!</p>\n<p><em>2211 Woodward Ave., +1 313-471-7000, foxtheatredetroit.net. Tours take place on Mondays, Thursdays, and Saturdays and go on sale two weeks in advance. Tickets are $20 USD for tours; ticket prices for performances vary. Check the website for details.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>8. Take a Tour of the Guardian Building</h3>\n<p>You’ll find many architectural beauties around Detroit, but the most prestigious is the 36-floor Guardian Building downtown, located in the Financial District. Completed in 1929, it is a National Historic Landmark and one of the most important Art Deco skyscrapers in the world!</p>\n<p><a href=\"http://detroitexperiencefactory.org/public-tours\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Detroit Experience Factory</a> offers a few free walking tours, including an Art and Architecture tour that covers the Guardian Building if you want to learn more during your visit.</p>\n<p><em>500 Griswold St., +1 313-963-4567, guardianbuilding.com. Open 24/7. Admission to the building is free.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>9. Walk Around Campus Martius Park</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit11.jpg\" alt=\"The Campus Martius Park in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nAfter a devastating fire in 1805, Campus Martius was created as the de facto center of Detroit’s rebuilding efforts. Covering just over an acre, the park features outdoor cafés and bars, a mini beach, green space, food trucks galore, monuments, and a host of weekend festivals and activities.</p>\n<p>In the winter, you’ll find a giant Christmas tree, an ice-skating rink, and a Christmas market. Every time I visit this area of town, I reflect on how far the city has come in the past ten years.</p>\n<p><em>To visit the park, take the light rail to the Campus Martius station.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>10. Snap Photos at The Belt</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit5.jpg\" alt=\"Street art in an alleyway in The Belt in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nThe Belt, named after its location in the former downtown garment district, is a culturally redefined alley in the heart of Detroit. Public art is the driving force behind the redevelopment of The Belt, which has murals and installations by local, national, and international artists. It is part of Library Street Collective’s continuous effort to ensure that artists have a space to create and engage with the public.</p>\n<p><em>To visit the Belt, take the light rail to Broadway station.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>11. See the Motown Museum</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit12.jpg\" alt=\"The exterior of the small Motown Museum in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nMotown Records is an R&#38;B and soul record label based in Detroit credited with advancing the racial integration of pop music in the 1960s and ’70s. Best-selling artists like the Temptations, the Four Tops, the Miracles, the Supremes, and many others were on the Motown label. (Motown is a portmanteau of “motor” and “town” since Detroit is known as Motor City.)</p>\n<p>Its main office, named Hitsville U.S.A., was converted into a museum in 1985 to highlight the important contributions of Motown to the greater American music scene. It has all sorts of records, awards, and costumes from famous musicians (including Michael Jackson). You can also see one of the recording studios where many of the label’s classic hits were produced.</p>\n<p><em>2648 W. Grand Blvd., +1 313-875-2264, motownmuseum.org. Open Tuesday–Sunday 10am–6pm (8pm on Saturdays). Admission is $15 USD.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>12. Henry Ford Museum of American Innovation</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit6.jpg\" alt=\"Old Ford automobiles in the Henry Ford museum in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nHenry Ford, a Michigan native and founder of the Ford Motor Company (and prominent anti-Semite), was responsible for kick-starting the automobile industry in the US in the early 1900s.</p>\n<p>Today, you can tour the company’s massive museum and learn about the history of the automobile and how it evolved from a novelty to a staple of modern society. The museum has numerous cars (including presidential automobiles), as well as exhibitions on trains, power generation, and much more.</p>\n<p>Additionally, adjacent to the museum is Greenfield Village, a semi-separate museum that hosts all kinds of science and agriculture exhibitions that Ford collected over his lifetime. It’s a great place to visit with kids, as many of the exhibits are interactive and educational.</p>\n<p><em>20900 Oakwood Blvd., Dearborn, +1 313-982-6001, thehenryford.org/visit/henry-ford-museum. Admission is $25 USD.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>13. Visit the Museum of African-American History</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/traveldetroit13.jpg\" alt=\"The exterior of the Charles H. Wright Museum of African-American History in Detroit, Michigan\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" /><br />\nOpened in 1965, this is the world’s biggest permanent collection of African-American culture. There are over 35,000 items and artifacts highlighting the history and culture of African-Americans throughout the ages. The museum has exhibitions on civil rights, art, film, and much more.</p>\n<p><em>315 E. Warren Ave., +1 313-494-5800, thewright.org. Open Tuesday–Sunday 9am–5pm and Sundays 1pm–5pm. Admission is $10 USD.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>14. Take a Food or Brewery Tour</h3>\n<p>Detroit is fast becoming a foodie destination. There are tons of delicious restaurants and a growing number of breweries here, kickstarting a foodie renaissance that is putting the city on the map. If you’re looking for an introduction into Detroit’s food and drink scene, take a tour. There are plenty of food and brewery tours that will give you a mouthwatering or thirst-quenching introduction to the culinary and microbrewery scenes.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.detroithistorytours.com/shop/order-up-the-detroit-food-tour\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Detroit History Tours</a> and <a href=\"https://detroitfoodietours.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Detroit Foodie Tours</a> both offer excellent and insightful food tours to some of the best restaurants, while <a href=\"https://www.motorcitybrewtours.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Motor City Brew Tours</a> will introduce you to the best beers Detroit has to offer. You’ll get to eat some wonderful food, try tasty drinks, and meet the chefs and restaurateurs making it all possible!<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>Where to Eat</h3>\n<p>If you’re looking for some places to grab a bite to eat, here are a few of my favorites:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>The Peterboro</strong> – Mouth watering and inventive Chinese food paired with craft beer and cocktails</li>\n<li><strong>SheWolf</strong> – Trendy and upscale Italian cuisine</li>\n<li><strong>Selden Standard</strong> – Locally grown and seasonal plates</li>\n<li><strong>Gold Cash Gold</strong> – Local food meets Mediterranean-inspired dishes</li>\n<li><strong>Bronx Bar</strong> – A classic dive bar with greasy eats</li>\n<li><strong>Sugar House</strong> – An intimate craft cocktail pub</li>\n<li><strong>Brooklyn Street Diner</strong> – A cozy diner with local food and lots of vegetarian options</li>\n</ul>\n<p><center>***</center>Detroit is one of the best up-and-coming cities in the country. With a developing food scene, an affordable cost of living, and more and more things opening each month, I suspect tourism here is only going to continue to grow. Come and visit while you can and beat the crowds. I promise Detroit will surpass your expectations!</p>\n<p><em>Raimee is the creative director for Nomadic Matt and runs the remote work and travel blog, <a href=\"https://www.doitallabroad.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">All Abroad</a>. She spent the past 4 years working remotely from cities around the world after leaving a marketing job in her hometown outside of Detroit, Michigan. She now resides in Los Angeles, California where she is social distancing but hopes to someday enjoy all of the comedy shows, live music, beaches, and hikes around the state! You can follow her remote work adventures on <a href=\"http://instagram.com/remoteraimee\">Instagram</a> and <a href=\"http://twitter.com/raimeeiacofano\">Twitter</a>.</em></p>\n<div class=\"single-booking\" style=\"clear: both;\">\n<h4>Book Your Trip to the United States: Logistical Tips and Tricks</h4>\n<p><strong>Book Your Flight</strong><br />\nFind a cheap flight by using <a href=\"http://www.anrdoezrs.net/click-3032045-12837948\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Skyscanner</a> or <a href=\"https://www.momondo.com/\">Momondo</a>. They are my two favorite search engines, because they search websites and airlines around the globe so you always know no stone is being left unturned.</p>\n<p><strong>Book Your Accommodation</strong><br />\nYou can book your hostel with <a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Hostelworld</a> as they have the largest inventory. If you want to stay somewher eother than a hotel, use <a href=\"https://www.booking.com/index.html?aid=362916\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Booking.com</a>, as they consistently return the cheapest rates for guesthouses and cheap hotels.</p>\n<p><strong>Don&#8217;t Forget Travel Insurance</strong><br />\nTravel insurance will protect you against illness, injury, theft, and cancellations. It&#8217;s comprehensive protection in case anything goes wrong. I never go on a trip without it, as I&#8217;ve had to use it many times in the past. />\n<strong>Posted</strong>: 7/20/20 | July 20th, 2020</p>\n<p>Last year, we launched <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/introducing-the-nomadic-network/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">The Nomadic Network</a>, a global travel initiative designed to connect travelers with like-minded people in their area in order to inspire, educate, and provide them with the tools they needed to travel cheaper, better, and longer. </p>\n<p>We wanted it to be a global travel club with chapters around the world. Something you could do in your own city but also one the road. (Want to make some local friends? Find a meetup where you&#8217;re going.) </p>\n<p>This video of The Nomadic Network NYC chapter launch will give you a feel for what they were like:</p>\n<p><iframe width=\"675\" height=\"395\" cities: <a href=\"https://thenomadicnetwork.com/chapters/austin\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Austin</a>, <a href=\"https://thenomadicnetwork.com/chapters/boston\" target=\"_blank\" In each city, we heard numerous stories about how happy members were to finally be able to exchange the travel stories they’d been dying to talk about with people who are just as excited to hear them. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate02.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"A group of travelers meeting at a Nomadic Network in-person event\"></p>\n<p>But, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/coronavirus-travel/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">when COVID made the world come to a screeching halt in March</a>, so did our events.</p>\n<p>Since then, however, we’ve been building up our online presence and launched three new endeavors to get people excited and involved:</p>\n<p><strong>First, we have been hosting virtual events.</strong> We’ve hosted discussions, speakers, Q&#038;As, and travel game sessions so far. These events offer a chance for people to connect with like-minded travelers from anywhere in the world. </p>\n<p>We’ve also hosted chapter-specific happy hours for people to get to know others in their vicinity while social distancing.</p>\n<p>By doing these events, we’re continuing our mission to connect travelers with people and resources that will have them traveling cheaper, better, and longer when the world opens up again. </p>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate01.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"A group of travelers meeting at a Nomadic Network in-person event\"></p>\n<p>If you’re interested in attending a virtual event, here are our talks:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://thenomadicnetwork.com/events/2020/07/visiting-bhutan-what-its-what-you-need-know\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Visiting Bhutan: What You Need to Know</a> (Tuesday, 7/21)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://thenomadicnetwork.com/events/2020/07/celebrate-flytes-five-year-anniversary\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Celebrate FLYTE&#8217;s Five Year Anniversary</a> (Wednesday, 7/22)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://thenomadicnetwork.com/events/2020/07/freelance-travel-writing-age-coronavirus\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Freelance Travel Writing in the Age of Coronavirus</a> (Thursday, 7/23)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://thenomadicnetwork.com/events/2020/07/whats-your-traveler-footprint-unpack-your-racialized-baggage\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">What&#8217;s Your Traveler Footprint? You’ll be able to ask questions, share your advice, and connect with other travelers! </p>\n<p>Now that Lonely Planet’s Thorntree is shut down, the world needs a travel forum. Our goal is to make this the best one out there! So come over to our forums <a href=\"https://forums.thenomadicnetwork.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">ask a question, share your knowledge, and meet some cool people in the process!</a> </p>\n<p><center>***</center></p>\n<p>We’re focused on growing The Nomadic Network online right now, creating a space where travelers can learn, meet people in their area, ask questions, or share knowledge. </p>\n<p>Best of all? They are my two favorite search engines, because they search websites and airlines around the globe so you always know no stone is being left unturned. </p>\n<p><strong>Book Your Accommodation</strong><br />\nYou can book your hostel with <a href=\"https://prf.hn/click/camref:1101lHtj\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Hostelworld</a> as they have the largest inventory. If you want to stay somewhere other than a hostel, use <a href=\"https://www.booking.com/index.html?aid=362916\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Booking.com</a>, as they consistently return the cheapest rates for guesthouses and hotels.</p>\n<p><strong>Don&#8217;t Forget Travel Insurance</strong><br />\nTravel insurance will protect you against illness, injury, theft, and cancellations. It&#8217;s comprehensive protection in case anything goes wrong. I never go on a trip without it, as I&#8217;ve had to use it many times in the past. I&#8217;ve been using <a href=\"https://www.worldnomads.com/lovedby/nomadic-matt?affiliate=nmts&#38;subId=brandlink&#38;affiliateTrackingId=0fe898ceef264503a88f74f4bbc7bb5b&#38;utm_source=nmts&#38;utm_medium=affiliate&#38;utm_campaign=Affiliate&#38;utm_content=brandlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads</a> for ten years. My favorite companies that offer the best service and value are:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.worldnomads.com/lovedby/nomadic-matt?affiliate=nmts&#38;subId=brandlink&#38;affiliateTrackingId=0fe898ceef264503a88f74f4bbc7bb5b&#38;utm_source=nmts&#38;utm_medium=affiliate&#38;utm_campaign=Affiliate&#38;utm_content=brandlink\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">World Nomads</a> (for everyone below 70)</li>\n<li><a href=https://www.insuremytrip.com/?linkId=5d1cb667a65d12041eca44ec\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Insure My Trip</a> (for those 70 and over)</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://medjetassist.com/nomadicmatt\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Medjet</a> (for additional repatriation coverage)</li>\n</ul>\n<p><strong>Looking for the best companies to save money with?</strong><br />\nCheck out my <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-resources/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">resource page</a> for the best companies to use when you travel! I list all those I use — and they&#8217;ll save you time and money too! </p>\n</div>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-blogs/nomadic-network-update/\">The Nomadic Network: A Community Update &#038; More Events</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com\">Nomadic Matt&#039;s Travel Site</a>.</p>\n","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 20 Jul 2020 09:51:09 +0000","feedId":55,"bgimg":"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate05.jpg","linkMd5":"c12c6bd7fa555849e05037fffdd8bd51","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn32@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596109818851_68a3f183973c46be.webp","author":"NomadicMatt","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate05.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn32@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596109818851_68a3f183973c46be.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate02.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn92@2020_4/2020-07-30/1596109829990_7f856e965e096dbf.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate01.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn54@2020_3/2020-07-30/1596109829876_fd1f545c4d5ebb74.webp","https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/thenomadicnetworkupdate04.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn40@2020_1/2020-07-30/1596109830401_8213a0469e0e4715.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-07-30 19:50:09","updatedTime":"2020-07-30 11:50:09","title":"The 18 Best Things to See and Do in Oslo","link":"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/?p=205353","description":"<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings1.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"Downtown Oslo, Norway in the summer near the water\"><br />\n<strong>Posted</strong>: 6/26/2020 | June 26th, 2020</p>\n<p>Balancing natural beauty, historic architecture, and a modern flair, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-guides/norway-travel-tips/oslo/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Oslo</a>, Norway&#8217;s beautiful capital, is a city that punches well above its weight. It&#8217;s small but bursting with things to do: there are countless museums, spacious parks, and delicious restaurants to keep you busy. It&#8217;s close to numerous forests with hiking and biking trails, and there are lots of places to swim in the summer too.</p>\n<p>However, since it&#8217;s expensive, a lot of travelers pass Oslo by. </p>\n<p>While a visit will certainly eat into your budget, Oslo is definitely worth a couple of days. To show precisely why you should add it to your itinerary, here are my favorite things to see and do there:<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>1. Take a Free Walking Tour</h3>\n<p>I always start my trips off with a free walking tour. They&#8217;re a budget-friendly way to see the main sights, learn a little history, meet other travelers, and get access to an expert local guide who can answer all my questions.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://freetouroslo.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Free Tour Oslo</a> organizes daily tours that last 90 minutes and are a solid introduction to the city. Make sure to tip the guide when the tour ends!<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>2. Enjoy the View at the Opera House</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings1d.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The famous Oslo Opera House in Norway overlooking the city at sunset\"><br />\nThis massive contemporary theatre sits on the water and is home to the national opera and ballet. Opened in 2007, the building itself is composed of multiple flat levels that essentially act as small plazas, allowing visitors to walk on the roof and enjoy the view of the harbor and city. It&#8217;s a popular spot to watch the sunset when the weather is nice. Check the website to see what performances are happening during your visit.</p>\n<p><em>Kirsten Flagstads Plass 1, +47 21 42 21 21, operaen.no. Open weekdays 10am–7pm, Saturdays 11am–6pm, and Sundays 12pm–6pm. Admission to performances varies.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>3. See the Viking Museum</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/48osloviking.jpg\" alt=\"Viking Museum, Oslo\" /><br />\nLocated on Bygdøy Island, this museum is home to some of the best-preserved Viking longships in the world. These ships, some of which date back to the ninth century, were found around Norway. There are Viking burial ships here, as well as carts and tools from the Middle Ages. The museum is relatively sparse (the highlight really is the ships), but there is also a short film and some other interesting tidbits of information. Download the free audio guide to make the most of your visit.</p>\n<p><em>Huk Aveny 35, +47 22 13 52 80, khm.uio.no/besok-oss/vikingskipshuset. Open daily 9am–6pm in the summer and 10am–4pm in the winter. Admission is 120 NOK ($12.40 USD) for adults and free for kids under 18.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>4. Explore the Vigeland Sculpture Park</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings2.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"A small baby statue in Vigeland Park in Oslo, Norway\"><br />\nThis unique collection of sculptures is located in Frogner Park. It&#8217;s the world’s largest display of sculptures created by a single artist. Gustav Vigeland (1869–1943) created over 200 bronze, iron, and granite statues that now stand in the open-air “gallery” (which covers a whopping 80 acres). The crying baby statue is the most famous. In the summer, there are often events held here as well.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>5. Visit the Historical Museum</h3>\n<p>This museum has lots of artifacts and information about Norway&#8217;s past, including lots of Viking content. There are also exhibitions on ancient Egypt (including mummies), Africa, and the country&#8217;s arctic expeditions. You can also use your ticket to get free entry to the Viking Museum (and vice versa) if done within 48 hours.</p>\n<p><em> Frederiks gate 2, +47 22 85 19 00, khm.uio.no/besok-oss/historisk-museum. Open Tuesday-Sunday 10am–4pm. Admission is 120 NOK ($12.40 USD) for adults and free for kids under 18. Free on the last Saturday of each month.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>6. Wander Holmenkollen</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings1a.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"A wintery view of Oslo, Norway and the old Olympic ski jump in Holmenkollen\"><br />\nThe Holmenkollen Ski Jump can be seen from every corner of the city. If you want to see it up close, hop on the subway and get off at the Holmenkollen stop. You can walk up to the jump and visit the Ski Jump Museum (the oldest ski museum in the world) if you want to learn more about this seemingly insane sport. The jump at Holmenkollen is 60 meters high, and there&#8217;s room for some 70,000 spectators (Norwegians love their winter sports!).</p>\n<p>From here you can also wander into Nordmarka for a hike. It&#8217;s a forested area north of the city, with hiking, walking, and cycling trails.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>7. Relax at Aker Brygge</h3>\n<p>Located near the pier, the largest concentration of restaurants in Oslo can be found here, ranging from French cuisine to traditional Nordic dishes. There&#8217;s lots of window-shopping and architecture to admire as well. The wharf has something for everyone and is a great place to spend a couple of hours soaking up life in the city.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>8. See the Royal Palace</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings3.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The historic Royal Palace in Oslo, Norway in the summer\"><br />\nThe official residence of the monarch, completed in the 1840s, is surrounded by a huge park, and locals can usually be seen in it enjoying what little sun they get throughout the year. During the summer, parts of the palace are also open to visitors. Tours last one hour, and you&#8217;ll be able to see some of the lavish historical rooms and learn about the monarchs who ruled Norway over the centuries.</p>\n<p><em>Slottsplassen 1, +47 22 04 87 00, kongehuset.no/seksjon.html. Summer hours vary. See the website for details. Admission is 140 NOK ($14.50 USD) and includes a tour. </em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>9. Visit the Norwegian Folk Museum</h3>\n<p>The Norsk Folkemuseum (Norwegian Museum of Cultural History) is an open-air museum home to relocated traditional Norwegian buildings. It&#8217;s near the Viking Ship Museum (and several other museums) and showcases over 150 buildings from various periods throughout Norwegian history. The most stunning of its exhibitions is Gol Stave Church, an intricately carved wooden church that dates back to the 13th century. In the summer, you can also feed animals and take carriage rides around the area. </p>\n<p><em>Museumsveien 10, +47 22 12 37 00, norskfolkemuseum.no. Open daily 11am–4pm. Admission is 160 NOK ($16.50 USD).</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>10. Go Swimming</h3>\n<p>Oslo is surrounded by water and nature. It&#8217;s a green capital, too, with many outdoor areas that are ideal for swimming in the summer; the water is clean and safe. Tjuvholmen City Beach, Sørenga Seawater Pool, and Huk are three spots worth checking out if you’re looking to take a dip when the weather is nice.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>11. Visit the Fram Museum</h3>\n<p>This unique museum focuses on polar exploration, a prevalent topic in Norwegian history and culture. The centerpiece is the Fram, the world’s first ice-breaking ship (and the last one made out of wood). In use between 1893 and 1912, the Fram made many trips to the North and South Poles and is said to have sailed farther north and farther south than any other wooden ship in history. The museum is very detailed and offers a lot of history, as well as photographs, tools, and artifacts. </p>\n<p><em>Bygdøynesveien 39, +47 23 28 29 50, frammuseum.no. Open daily 10am–6pm. Admission is 120 NOK ($12.40 USD).</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>12. Learn about the Kon-Tiki Expedition</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings5.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The famous Kon-Tiki balsa raft in Oslo, Norway\"><br />\nThe Kon-Tiki Museum focuses on the works and expeditions of historian and explorer Thor Heyerdahl. Using a traditional balsa raft, Heyerdahl crossed the Pacific Ocean from South America to Polynesia, proving that the islands were populated from the Americas and not Asia, as had been previously thought. The journey took 101 days and was filmed, winning an Academy Award in 1951.</p>\n<p>The original raft can be found in the museum, along with information on Heyerdahl&#8217;s other expeditions and archeological discoveries. The journey inspired the 2012 historical drama film <em>Kon-Tiki</em> (which is an excellent travel film).</p>\n<p><em>Bygdøynesveien 36, +47 23 08 67 67, kon-tiki.no. Open daily 9:30am–6pm (shorter hours in the autumn and winter). Admission is 120 NOK ($12.40 USD).</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>13. Visit the Holocaust Center</h3>\n<p>The Holocaust Center focuses on the Norwegian experience of the Holocaust, highlighting the tragic fate of Norwegian Jews, as well as the persecution of religious minorities around the world. Established in 2001, it moved from the university to the former residence of Vidkun Quisling, a fascist who headed the Norwegian government under Nazi occupation from 1942 to 1945 (he was executed for war crimes after the war ended). The center has all sorts of exhibitions, photos, films, artifacts, and interviews. It&#8217;s somber and sobering but worth a visit.</p>\n<p><em>Huk Aveny 56, +47 23 10 62 00, hlsenteret.no. Open weekdays 9am–4pm. Admission is 70 NOK ($7.25 USD).</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>14. See City Hall</h3>\n<p>Oslo&#8217;s City Hall is open to the public and free to enter. While it might not sound like an interesting sight, the tours are actually quite informative. There are about 20 murals and works of art inside the building, covering everything from traditional Norwegian life to the Nazi occupation. You&#8217;ll also learn about the history of the Nobel Peace Prize, which is awarded here annually (the other Nobel Prizes are awarded in <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-guides/sweden-travel-tips/stockholm/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Stockholm</a>).<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>15. Explore Akershus Castle</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings4.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The exterior of Akershus Castle in Oslo, Norway in the summer\"><br />\nOriginally built in 1290, Akershus Castle is a medieval fortress that later evolved into a Renaissance palace under Danish King Christian IV. It was built both for protection and as a royal residence (currently, it&#8217;s used as an office for the prime minister). The fortress has never successfully been besieged, though it did surrender to the Nazis during World War II. </p>\n<p>Inside, you&#8217;ll find a military museum, as well as one dedicated to the resistance during WWII. Guided tours are available in the summer, and there are often concerts and events held here, so check the website to see if anything is occurring during your visit.</p>\n<p><em>+47 23 09 39 17, forsvarsbygg.no/no/festningene/finn-din-festning/akershus-festning. Open daily in the summers 10am–4pm (winter hours vary). Admission is free.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>16. Go Tobogganing at Korketrekkeren</h3>\n<p>Tobogganing is a traditional Norwegian winter activity and one you shouldn&#8217;t miss if you&#8217;re here during the winter. Stretching over 2,000 meters long, the Korketrekkeren track is fun for adults and kids alike. Sleds and helmets are available for rent for 150 NOK ($15.50 USD), and you rent them by the day so you can enjoy as many rides as you like.<br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>17. Explore the National Gallery</h3>\n<p>The National Gallery contains Edvard Munch&#8217;s famous painting &#8220;The Scream&#8221; (which was made in 1893 and has been stolen twice). The gallery is small, but there are a lot of artists on display. You&#8217;ll find some Impressionist and Dutch works, as well as some by Picasso and El Greco. It&#8217;s not the most expansive collection, but it is still worth a visit, especially if you&#8217;re a fan of more classical art styles (like me).</p>\n<p><em>The National Gallery is temporarily closed and will reopen in 2021. You can find some of its collection in the National Museum.</em><br />\n&#160;</p>\n<h3>18. Get Off the Beaten Path</h3>\n<p><img src=\"https://media.nomadicmatt.com/2020/oslothings6.jpg\" width=\"675\" height=\"395\" alt=\"The She Lies sculpture in Oslo, Norway\"><br />\nIf you&#8217;re looking for something more quirky, here are some of the more off-the-beaten path sights and museums the city has to offer:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><strong>Norwegian Museum of Magic</strong> &#8211; A small museum open only on Sundays, with lots of magic memorabilia and props. There&#8217;s also a small magic performance.</li>\n<li><strong>The Devil of Oslo</strong> &#8211; This statue is over 900 years old and depicts a man being attacked by a lion and a dragon. It&#8217;s a part of the Oslo Cathedral (though it&#8217;s much older than the cathedral itself).</li>\n<li><strong>The Mini Bottle Gallery</strong> &#8211; Home to over 50,000 bottles, this quirky museum is the only mini bottle museum in the world. The bottles are filled with everything from food to bones to worms (and much, much more!).</li>\n<li><strong>Edvard Munch&#8217;s grave</strong> &#8211; Munch painted the famous &#8220;The Scream.&#8221;</li>\n<li><strong>She Lies</strong> &#8211; This glass and stainless steel sculpture sits out in the harbor and evokes the power and beauty of an iceberg.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>&#160;</p>\n<p><center>***</center></p>\n<p>No matter what you&#8217;re interested in, <a href=\"https://www.nomadicmatt.com/travel-guides/norway-travel-tips/oslo/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Oslo</a> will be able to keep you entertained. With its diverse collection of museums, an abundance of parks and hiking trails, and beautiful vistas and scenery, you&#8217;ll have a hard time getting bored here. And while it is an expensive destination, I promise that a trip to Oslo is worth every krone!</p>\n<div class=\"single-booking\">\n<h4>Book Your Trip to Oslo: Logistical Tips and Tricks</h4>\n<p><strong>Book Your Flight</strong><br />\nFind a cheap flight by using <a href=\"http://www.anrdoezrs.net/click-3032045-12837948\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Skyscanner</a> or <a href=\"https://www.momondo.com/\">Momondo</a>. If you want to stay somewhere else, use <a href=\"https://www.booking.com/index.html?aid=362916\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Booking.com</a>, as it consistently returns the cheapest rates for guesthouses and hotels.
